<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41477"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome structure and evolution of Antirrhinum majus L"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Plants 2019 5:2"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-01-28"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s), under exclusive licence to Springer Nature Limited"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Snapdragon (Antirrhinum majus L.), a member of the Plantaginaceae family, is an important model for plant genetics and molecular studies on plant growth and development, transposon biology and self-incompatibility. Here we report a near-complete genome assembly of A. majus cultivar JI7 (A. majus cv.JI7) comprising 510&#8201;Megabases (Mb) of genomic sequence and containing 37,714 annotated protein-coding genes. Scaffolds covering 97.12% of the assembled genome were anchored on eight chromosomes. Comparative and evolutionary analyses revealed that a whole-genome duplication event occurred in the Plantaginaceae around 46&#8211;49 million years ago (Ma). We also uncovered the genetic architectures associated with complex traits such as flower asymmetry and self-incompatibility, identifying a unique duplication of TCP family genes dated to around 46&#8211;49 Ma and reconstructing a near-complete &#968;S-locus of roughly 2&#8201;Mb. The genome sequence obtained in this study not only provides a representative genome sequenced from the Plantaginaceae but also brings the popular plant model system of Antirrhinum into the genomic age. This study reports a near-complete genome of Antirrhinum majus L., provides insights into gene and genome duplication events, and reveals the genetic basis underlying complex traits of snapdragon, such as flower asymmetry and self-incompatibility."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Snapdragon (<i>Antirrhinum majus</i> L.), a member of the Plantaginaceae family, is an important model for plant genetics and molecular studies on plant growth and development, transposon biology and self-incompatibility. Here we report a near-complete genome assembly of <i>A. majus</i> cultivar JI7 (<i>A. majus</i> cv.JI7) comprising 510 Megabases (Mb) of genomic sequence and containing 37,714 annotated protein-coding genes. Scaffolds covering 97.12% of the assembled genome were anchored on eight chromosomes. Comparative and evolutionary analyses revealed that a whole-genome duplication event occurred in the Plantaginaceae around 46–49 million years ago (Ma). We also uncovered the genetic architectures associated with complex traits such as flower asymmetry and self-incompatibility, identifying a unique duplication of TCP family genes dated to around 46–49 Ma and reconstructing a near-complete <i>ψS</i>-locus of roughly 2 Mb. The genome sequence obtained in this study not only provides a representative genome sequenced from the Plantaginaceae but also brings the popular plant model system of <i>Antirrhinum</i> into the genomic age.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The genus <i>Antirrhinum</i> belongs to the family Plantaginaceae and includes about 20 species with the chromosome number of 2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 16. <i>Antirrhinum</i> originated in Europe and is mainly distributed in Europe, Asia and Africa around the Mediterranean coast. Different species in the genus <i>Antirrhinum</i> exhibit differences in flower colour, flower pattern, fragrance and flowering time; interspecific hybridization has also been described. The genus exhibits two major mechanisms that promote outcrossing: insect pollination (entomophily) and self-incompatibility<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Schwarz-Sommer, Z., Davies, B. &amp; Hudson, A. An everlasting pioneer: the story of Antirrhinum research. Nat. Rev. Genet. 4, 655–664 (2003)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d14004e840">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nettancourt, D. Incompatibility in Angiosperms (Springer, Berlin, 1977)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d14004e840_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Franklin-Tong, V. E. Self-Incompatibility in Flowering Plants (Springer, Berlin, 2008)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d14004e843">3</a></sup>. The self-compatible <i>A. majus</i> was domesticated as a garden ornamental over 2,000 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Schwarz-Sommer, Z., Davies, B. &amp; Hudson, A. An everlasting pioneer: the story of Antirrhinum research. Nat. Rev. Genet. 4, 655–664 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d14004e850">1</a></sup>.</p><p><i>Antirrhinum</i> has served as a model system for molecular and developmental genetics for the past three decades<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Schwarz-Sommer, Z., Davies, B. &amp; Hudson, A. An everlasting pioneer: the story of Antirrhinum research. Nat. Rev. Genet. 4, 655–664 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d14004e859">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Coen, E. S., Carpenter, R. &amp; Martin, C. Transposable elements generate novel spatial patterns of gene expression in Antirrhinum majus. Cell 47, 285–296 (1986)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d14004e862">4</a></sup>. Several key floral genes were first identified in <i>Antirrhinum</i> including founding members of the MADS (<i>DEFICIENS</i>) and TCP (<i>CYCLOIDEA</i>) gene families, <i>MYB</i> genes controlling petal epidermal cell shape (<i>MIXTA</i>) or flower colour (<i>ROSEA</i> and <i>VENOSA</i>) and <i>SLF</i>s (<i>S</i>-<i>L</i>ocus <i>F</i>-box) controlling self-incompatibility<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sommer, H., Beltrán, J. P. &amp; Huijser, P. Deficiens, a homeotic gene involved in the control of flower morphogenesis in Antirrhinum majus: the protein shows homology to transcription factors. EMBO J. 9, 605–613 (1990)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d14004e901">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Tröbner, W., Ramirez, L. &amp; Motte, P. GLOBOSA: a homeotic gene which interacts with DEFICIENS in the control of Antirrhinum floral organogenesis. EMBO J. 11, 4693–4704 (1992)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d14004e901_1">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Luo, D., Carpenter, R., Vincent, C., Copsey, L. &amp; Coen, E. Origin of floral asymmetry in Antirrhinum. Nature 383, 794–799 (1996)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14004e901_2">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Luo, D. et al. Control of organ asymmetry in flowers of Antirrhinum. Cell 99, 367–376 (1999)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14004e901_3">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Glover, B. J., Perez-Rodrigues, M. &amp; Martin, C. Development of several epidermal cell types can be specified by the same MYB-related plant transcription factor. Development 125, 3497–3508 (1998)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d14004e901_4">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Schwinn, K. et al. A small family of MYB-regulatory genes controls floral pigmentation intensity and patterning in the genus Antirrhinum. Plant Cell 18, 831–851 (2006)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d14004e901_5">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lai, Z. et al. An F-box gene linked to the self-incompatibility (S) locus of Antirrhinum is expressed specifically in pollen and tapetum. Plant Mol. Biol. 50, 29–42 (2002)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d14004e901_6">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Qiao, H. et al. The F-box protein AhSLF-S2 controls the pollen function of S-RNase-based self-incompatibility. Plant Cell 16, 2307–2322 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d14004e904">12</a></sup>. Isolation and analysis of genes in <i>Antirrhinum</i> have been facilitated by the availability of endogenous active transposons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Schwarz-Sommer, Z., Davies, B. &amp; Hudson, A. An everlasting pioneer: the story of Antirrhinum research. Nat. Rev. Genet. 4, 655–664 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d14004e911">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Hudson, A., Critchley, J. &amp; Erasmus, Y. The genus Antirrhinum (snapdragon): a flowering plant model for evolution and development. Cold Spring Harb. Protoc. 3, 1–7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d14004e914">13</a></sup>. For example, five transposable elements (<i>Tam1</i>, <i>Tam2</i>, <i>Tam3</i>, <i>Tam4</i> and <i>Tam11</i>) <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nacken, W. K., Piotrowiak, R., Saedler, H. &amp; Sommer, H. The transposable element Tam1 from Antirrhinum majus shows structural homology to the maize transposon En/Spm and has no sequence specificity of insertion. Mol. Gen. Genet. 228, 201–208 (1991)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d14004e934">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Krebbers, E. et al. Molecular analysis of paramutant plants of Antirrhinum majus and the involvement of transposable elements. Mol. Gen. Genet. 209, 499–507 (1987)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d14004e934_1">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hehl, R., Nacken, W. K., Krause, A., Saedler, H. &amp; Sommer, H. Structural analysis of Tam3, a transposable element from Antirrhinum majus, reveals homologies to the Ac element from maize. Plant Mol. Biol. 16, 369–371 (1991)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d14004e934_2">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Luo, D., Coen, E. S., Doyle, S. &amp; Carpenter, R. Pigmentation mutants produced by transposon mutagenesis in Antirrhinum majus. Plant J. 1, 59–69 (1991)." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d14004e934_3">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Galego, L. &amp; Almeida, J. Role of DIVARICATA in the control of dorsoventral asymmetry in Antirrhinum flowers. Genes Dev. 16, 880–891 (2002)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d14004e937">18</a></sup> have contributed to the identification of floral homeotic genes. However, so far these studies have been carried out without the benefit of a genome sequence to provide an overall evolutionary and architectural context for these genes, transposons and traits.</p><p>Here we report a near-complete genome assembly of <i>A. majus</i>. We annotated 37,714 protein-coding genes on the basis of expression and homology evidence. The assembly was generated by combining whole-genome shotgun (WGS) sequencing of short reads on the Illumina platform and single-molecule real time (SMRT) long reads on the Pacific Biosciences (PacBio) platform. Most of the assembled sequences were anchored onto chromosomes to form eight pseudomolecules using a genetic map. Comparative analysis based on this sequence reveals that the Plantaginaceae and Solanaceae diverged from their most recent ancestor about 62 Ma, and that a whole-genome duplication (WGD) event occurred around 46–49 Ma. We found that the WGD contributed to the evolution of the TCP gene family related to flower asymmetry in <i>A. majus</i>. We also analysed the near-complete genomic structure of the pseudo (<i>ψ</i>) <i>S</i>-locus of <i>A. majus</i> of roughly 2 Mb, which contained 102 genes including 37 SLF genes. The genome sequence provided here will accelerate genomic and evolutionary studies in this model species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing, assembly and annotation of <i>A. majus</i>
                        </h3><p>We sequenced a highly inbred <i>Antirrhinum</i> line (<i>A. majus</i> cv. JI7) using a combination of Illumina short-read and PacBio long-read sequencing technologies. The genome size was estimated from <i>k</i>-mer distributions to be about 520 Mb. We obtained 90.85 gigabases (Gb) high-quality Illumina paired-end reads, equivalent to 174-fold sequence coverage of the genome. We used CANU<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Koren, S., Walenz, B. P., Berlin, K., Miller, J. R. &amp; Phillippy, A. M. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d14004e984">19</a></sup> to correct and assemble 25.89 Gb PacBio reads into contigs and SSPACE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Marten, B. &amp; Walter, P. SSPACE-LongRead: scaffolding bacterial draft genomes using long read sequence information. BMC Bioinformatics 15, 211 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d14004e988">20</a></sup> for scaffolding with Mate-paired short reads. The assembled genome size was 510 Mb with contig and scaffold N50 (the size above which 50% of the total length of the sequence assembly can be found) sizes of 0.73 and 2.6 Mb, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#Fig1">1</a>, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#Tab1">1</a>, Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM1"> 1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM1">1</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM1">4</a>). The mapping ratio of ×42.22 Illumina data was 99.55% (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM1">5</a>) and the coverage of contigs using PacBio data was 99.97%. The heterozygosity of the assembled genome was estimated to be 51 single nucleotide polymorphs (SNPs) per 1 Mb (0.0051%) by using Illumina sequencing data.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="An overview of the genomic features of A. majus JI7."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: An overview of the genomic features of <i>A. majus</i> JI7.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0349-9/MediaObjects/41477_2018_349_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0349-9/MediaObjects/41477_2018_349_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="670" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Roman numerals refer to: I, duplications of genomic paralogous sequences; II, guanine-cytosine (GC) content; III, simple sequence repeats; IV, gene density; V, retroelement density; VI, recombination rate (deep colour shows high recombination rates) and VII, eight <i>Antirrhinum</i> chromosomes with physical distances including low copy number repetitive elements: telomere repeat <i>TTTAGGG</i> (green), 5S recombinant DNA (orange) and pericentromeric repeats <i>CentA</i><i>1</i> and <i>CentA</i><i>2</i> (purple). A ruler with marks every 1 Mb is drawn on each chromosome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics for the <i>Antirrhinum</i> genome and gene annotation</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To anchor the <i>A. majus</i> genome sequence to chromosomes, we created linkage maps by re-sequencing 48 recombinant inbred lines (RILs) derived from <i>A. majus</i> crossed to the self-incompatible species <i>A. charidemi</i>. We identified a total of 4,523,444 homozygous SNPs between the parents on 1,386 contigs and obtained 4,198,995 SNPs on 1,381 contigs for linkage map construction. After validations by known genetic markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Schwarz-Sommer, Z. et al. A molecular recombination map of Antirrhinum majus. BMC Plant Biol. 10, 275 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d14004e1315">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Feng, X. et al. Evolution of allometry in Antirrhinum. Plant Cell 21, 2999–3007 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d14004e1318">22</a></sup>, 496.9 Mb (97.12%) of the assembled scaffold sequences were anchored onto eight linkage groups to form pseudomolecules. The pseudomolecules ranged in size from 50.9 to 75.4 Mb. The average recombination rate was 1.798 centimorgans per Mb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM1">6</a> and Supplementary Data Set <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM3">1</a>). The relationship between genetic and physical distances revealed significantly lower recombination rates at the centromere regions of all chromosomes and the extended pericentromeric regions of chromosomes 4, 6, 7 and 8. The linkage groups were linked to the physical chromosomes through fluorescence in situ hybridization (FISH) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM1">2</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM1">4</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><p>To evaluate the assembled genome quality, we aligned 25,651 expressed sequence tags (ESTs) of <i>Antirrhinum</i> downloaded from National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) nucleotide database to the assembled genome, and found that 96.59% of the ESTs could be mapped. Alignments between the assembled genome and three sequenced Bacterial Artificial Chromosomes (BACs) indicated an average nucleotide accuracy of 99.65% in the assembly. BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Simão, F. A. et al. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d14004e1344">23</a></sup> analysis showed 93.88% complete genes at the genome mode and 93.40% at the protein mode, which suggested that the quality of the assembled <i>Antirrhinum</i> genome sequence was comparable to that of other published plant genomes (<i>Petunia</i> and <i>Arabidopsis</i>) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM1">5</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM1">7</a> and Supplementary Data Set <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM3">2</a>). Taken together, these results suggested that the <i>A. majus</i> genome assembly was both highly accurate and near completion.</p><p>We predicted a total of 37,714 protein-coding genes with an average transcript length of 3,166 base pairs (bp) by using a combination of ab initio and evidence-based methods<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W435–W439 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d14004e1373">24</a></sup>. We used <i>Antirrhinum</i> EST sequences and RNA-seq data from six major tissues: leaf, root, stem, stamen, pistil and pollen (Supplementary Data Set <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM3">3</a>) to confirm the expression of the genes. Approximately 89% of the genes were functionally annotated. The average gene density in <i>Antirrhinum</i> was one gene per 15.5 kilobase (kb), which is about three times lower than <i>Arabidopsis</i> (one gene per 4.5 kb) and slightly higher than tomato (one gene per 25.7 kb). Genes were distributed unevenly, being more abundant towards the ends of the chromosomal arms (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#Fig1">1</a>). We identified genes encoding 981 transfer RNAs, 800 microRNAs, 10 ribosomal RNAs (18S, 28S, 5.8S and 5S) and 622 small nuclear RNAs. A total of 268.3 Mb (52.6%) of sequences was annotated as repeats, including a wealth of class I (retrotransposon: 182.8 Mb) and class II (DNA transposon: 41.1 Mb) elements (Supplementary Tables  <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM1">7</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM1">10</a>).</p><p>We found 95 transposable elements belonging to the <i>En</i>/<i>Spm</i>/<i>CACTA</i> family. Three subfamilies (<i>Tam2</i>, <i>Tam4</i> and <i>Tam11</i>) had copies with 100% identity, suggesting recent duplication/transposition events. We also identified 166.21 Mb comprising long-terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons, with sequence similarity between copies indicating a mean divergence time of ~0.86 Ma. Bursts of <i>Gypsy</i> and <i>Copia</i> retrotransposon insertions were detected at 0.1–0.2 Ma and 120–130 Ma, respectively. These results suggest that the <i>Antirrhinum</i> genome has a long history of active transposition (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM1">11</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM1">8</a> and Supplementary Data Set <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM3">4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Comparative genomic analysis of <i>A. majus</i>
                        </h3><p>Self-alignment analysis revealed duplicated and triplicated regions between and within chromosomes. Paralogous relationships among the eight <i>Antirrhinum</i> chromosomes revealed 45 major duplications and two triplications, collectively containing 1,841 pairs of paralogous genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#Fig1">1</a> and Supplementary Data Set <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM3">5</a>). We performed all-against-all comparisons to identify 2,115 single-copy genes of <i>Antirrhinum</i> with orthologues in nine angiosperm species (<i>A. majus</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Amborella trichopoda</i>, <i>Carica papaya</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Petunia hybrida</i>, <i>Prunus mume</i>, <i>Solanium lycopersicum</i>, <i>Symphytum tuberosum</i> and <i>Vitis vinifera</i>). The resulting phylogenetic tree (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#Fig2">2a</a>) show that the <i>Antirrhinum</i> lineage split from potato and tomato lineages around 62 Ma, consistent with the results of Bell et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bell, C. D., Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. The age and diversification of the angiosperms re-revisited. Am. J. Bot. 97, 1296–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d14004e1503">25</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Genome evolution of A. majus."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Genome evolution of <i>A. majus</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0349-9/MediaObjects/41477_2018_349_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0349-9/MediaObjects/41477_2018_349_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="786" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Phylogenetic tree of angiosperm species including their divergence time on the basis of orthologues of single-gene families. The red star highlights the genome duplication in the <i>A. majus</i> lineage. The number in each node indicates Ma between two divergent branches. Green/red numbers indicate expansion and contraction gene families. <i>A. trichopoda</i> was used as an outgroup. Bootstrap values for each node are above 100%. <b>b</b>, Synteny blocks among chromosomes of <i>A. majus</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i> and <i>S. lycopersicum</i>. The numbers represent individual chromosomes. The selected syntenic gene numbers are more than 50 in each block. <b>c</b>, Density distributions of Ks for paralogous genes among <i>A. majus</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i> and <i>S. lycopersicum</i>. <b>d</b>, A Venn diagram of shared orthologues among four species of <i>A. majus</i>, <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>O. sativa</i>. Each number represents a gene family number shared among the genomes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>For inter-species comparative genomic studies, we examined the synteny of <i>Antirrhinum</i> chromosomes and those of <i>V. vinifera</i> and <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>. We found only small syntenic blocks between the <i>A. majus</i> and the <i>V. vinifera</i> or <i>S. lycopersicum</i> chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#Fig2">2b</a>). We also compared <i>A. majus</i> with <i>Sesamum indicum</i>, <i>Olea europea</i>, <i>Helianthus annuus</i> and <i>Coffea arabica</i>, which all belong to Lamiales. Large syntenic blocks were found between the <i>Antirrhinum</i> genome and these species, especially between <i>Antirrhinum</i> chromosomes 1, 2, 6 and 8 and <i>C. arabica</i> chromosomes 3, 1/1, 4/7, and 6, respectively (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM1">9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM1">10</a>).</p><p>We identified the syntenic blocks within the <i>A. majus</i> genome through intragenome comparisons. We calculated the density distribution of synonymous substitution rate per gene (Ks) between the collinear paralogous genes and inferred paleotetraploidy event in <i>Antirrhinum</i>. A peak at around 0.57–0.60 indicated that a WGD, which was Plantaginaceae-specific, occurred around 46–49 Ma (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#Fig2">2c</a>), clearly after the divergence of <i>Antirrhinum</i> lineage from the lineage of potato and tomato.</p><p>We then compared the complexity of gene families between <i>Antirrhinum</i> and other species: 9,503 gene families were shared by <i>Antirrhinum</i>, <i>Arabidopsis</i>, rice and tomato; 6,677 gene families were possibly contracted in <i>Antirrhinum</i>, while the other 3,778 gene families were expanded (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#Fig2">2d</a>). Gene-set enrichment analysis (GSEA) analysis results showed that gene families encoding protein kinase activity, catalytic activity, transporter activity and ATP-binding activity were most obviously expanded (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM1">12</a>). Transcription factor gene families, such as AP2, C2H2, GRAS, TCP and Trihelix, were expanded in species belonging to the order Lamiales (<i>A. majus</i>, <i>S. indicum</i> and <i>O. europea</i>) compared with <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>S. lycopersicum</i> and <i>V. vitis</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM1">13</a> and Supplementary Data Set <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM3">6</a>).</p><p>We analysed the contributions of tandem duplication and WGD to expansions/contractions. We found most F-box domain (PF00646), cytochrome P450 (PF00067) and NB-ARC domain (PF00931) gene families were derived from tandem duplication events (percentage of tandem duplication genes &gt;40%, compared with the genome average 12.1%). The percentage of expanded transcription factor genes caused by WGD (27.5%) was larger than the genome average (16.4%), especially for WRKY (49.2%), MADS-MIKC (43.3%), bZIP (39.1%), TCP (37.5%) and G2-like GARP (36.8%) (Supplementary Data Sets <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM3">6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM3">7</a>). Thus, both WGD and tandem duplication have played an important role in the expansion of gene families.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Evolution of floral asymmetry and TCP family</h3><p><i>A. majus</i> has served as the genetic model of floral symmetry. Previous studies have revealed that floral asymmetry in <i>A. majus</i> is largely controlled by two transcripion factors (TFs) (<i>CYC</i> and <i>DICH</i>) that belong to the TCP gene family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Luo, D., Carpenter, R., Vincent, C., Copsey, L. &amp; Coen, E. Origin of floral asymmetry in Antirrhinum. Nature 383, 794–799 (1996)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14004e1728">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Luo, D. et al. Control of organ asymmetry in flowers of Antirrhinum. Cell 99, 367–376 (1999)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14004e1731">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Cubas, P., Lauter, N., Doebley, J. &amp; Coen, E. The TCP domain: a motif found in proteins regulating plant growth and development. Plant J. 18, 215–222 (1999)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d14004e1734">26</a></sup>. To explore their evolution, we analysed the composition of the TCP families in <i>A. majus</i> and several sequenced angiosperms with floral symmetry. The TCP family is divided into two classes, class I (PCF) and II, and class II is further divided into two clades, CIN and CYC/TB1. Both eudicot and monocot share a subfamily containing <i>CYC</i> and <i>DICH</i> genes that belong to the CYC/TB1 clade. However, the basal angiosperm <i>A. trichopoda</i>, which has radially symmetrical flowers, lacks any members of the class II CYC/TB1 clade. Two monocots and several eudicots also have radially symmetrical flowers (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#Fig3">3</a>). These findings suggest that the TCP class II CYC/TB1 clade<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Cubas, P., Lauter, N., Doebley, J. &amp; Coen, E. The TCP domain: a motif found in proteins regulating plant growth and development. Plant J. 18, 215–222 (1999)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d14004e1754">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Cubas, P. in Developmental Genetics and Plant Evolution (eds Cronk, Q. C. B. et al.) Ch. 13 (Taylor &amp; Francis, London, 2002)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d14004e1757">27</a></sup> appeared after the emergence of radially symmetrical flowers, and the initial role of CYC/TB1 clade was thus not likely to be involved in the control of floral symmetry (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#Fig3">3</a> and Supplementary Data Set <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM3">8</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Evolution of flower symmetry and TCP gene family."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Evolution of flower symmetry and TCP gene family.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0349-9/MediaObjects/41477_2018_349_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0349-9/MediaObjects/41477_2018_349_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="336" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Left, a phylogenetic tree of the flowering plants derived from their divergence time based on orthologues of single-gene families. Three red stars show the whole-genome triplication and two green stars the duplication events (<a href="http://chibba.agtec.uga.edu/duplication/">http://chibba.agtec.uga.edu/duplication/</a>). Right, B represents bilateral flower symmetry and R radial flower symmetry; Asterid, Rosid, Basal rosid, Basal eudicot, Monocot and Basal angiosperm represent the clade names, respectively. Total numbers of TCP family genes, Class I PCF, Class II CIN and Class II CYC/TB1 are shown from left to right. The asterisk indicates the sequenced genome of species of <i>Medicago truncatula</i> with flower radial symmetry, but flowers of most <i>Medicago</i> species have bilateral symmetry. The double asterisk indicates <i>Lotus corniculatus</i> in which three TCP genes were identified but a functional TCP gene was not detected in its genome. Four-pointed stars denote flowers with bilateral symmetry with their photos from PPBC (<a href="http://www.plantphoto.cn">http://www.plantphoto.cn</a>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We identified a total of 30 putative functional TCP family genes in <i>Antirrhinum</i>: 13 class I genes and 17 class II genes (10 in the CIN clade and 7 in the CYC/TB1 clade) (Supplementary Data Set <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM3">8</a>). Syntenic block and Ks analyses of the orthologous gene pairs revealed that both WGD and tandem duplication contributed to the expansion of TCP family members. A previous study showed that <i>CYC</i> and <i>DICH</i> have partial redundancy in the control of flower asymmetry and exhibit only partially similar expression patterns in floral meristems in <i>A. majus</i>, and the two genes act together to establish the flower asymmetry in <i>A. majus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Luo, D. et al. Control of organ asymmetry in flowers of Antirrhinum. Cell 99, 367–376 (1999)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14004e1832">8</a></sup>. We found that the <i>CYC</i> and <i>DICH</i> genes reside on a pair of syntenic regions including 79 homologous gene pairs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM1">14</a>). The Ks analysis results show that this syntenic block was retained from the Plantaginaceae-specific WGD event. Previous phylogenetic analysis suggested that zygomorphic flowers independently evolved from actinomorphic ancestors more than 25 times<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Raimundo, J. et al. A subcellular tug of war involving three MYB-like proteins underlies a molecular antagonism in Antirrhinum flower asymmetry. Plant J. 75, 527–538 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d14004e1845">28</a></sup>. On the basis of fossil records, it was proposed that clearly zygomorphic flowers emerged in various lineages roughly 50 Ma<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Reardon, W. et al. Different outcomes for the MYB floral symmetry genes DIVARICATA and RADIALIS during the evolution of derived actinomorphy in. Plantago. New Phytol. 202, 716–725 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d14004e1850">29</a></sup>, concurrent with the occurring time of the WGD event. These results suggest that the WGD to generate both <i>CYC</i> and <i>DICH</i> genes played a critical role in the evolution of zygomorphic flowers in the <i>Antirrhinum</i> lineage.</p><p>Furthermore, two MYB-class genes <i>RAD</i> and <i>DIV</i>, acting downstream of <i>CYC</i><i>/</i><i>DICH</i> in the control of floral symmetry, interact with the <i>DRIF</i> gene. The <i>DRIF</i> has homologous copies with similar Ks to <i>CYC</i>/<i>DICH</i>, and they are also located at a WGD-derived syntenic block <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Preston, J. C., Martinez, C. C. &amp; Hileman, L. C. Gradual disintegration of the floral symmetry gene network is implicated in the evolution of a wind-pollination syndrome. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 2343–2348 (2011)." href="#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d14004e1892">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Donoghue, M., Ree, R. &amp; Baum, D. Phylogeny and the evolution of flower symmetry in the Asteridae. Trends. Plant. Sci. 3, 311–317 (1998)." href="#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d14004e1892_1">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Zhong, J. &amp; Kellogg, E. A. Duplication and expression of CYC2-like genes in the origin and maintenance of corolla zygomorphy in Lamiales. New Phytol. 205, 852–868 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d14004e1895">32</a></sup>. These results further support the idea that the key regulators of floral asymmetry were retained from the genes generated by the WGD in <i>Antirrhinum</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Structure of the <i>ψS</i>-locus in <i>A. majus</i> and its gene collinearity in self-incompatible species</h3><p>In previous cytological investigations, we found that the <i>Antirrhinum S</i>-locus is located in a heterochromatin region on the short arm of chromosome 8 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Yang, Q., Zhang, D., Li, Q., Cheng, Z. &amp; Xue, Y. Heterochromatic and genetic features are consistent with recombination suppression of the self-incompatibility locus in Antirrhinum. Plant J. 51, 140–151 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d14004e1920">33</a></sup>). The cultivated species <i>A. majus</i> is self-compatible, carrying a pseudo (<i>ψ</i>)<i>S</i>-locus. Scanning the <i>A. majus</i> genome for conserved (FBA/FBK domain) of the <i>SLF</i> gene family revealed the presence of 37 <i>SLF</i> genes (<i>SLF1</i>–<i>SLF37</i>) located in the short arm of chromosome 8, probably corresponding to the <i>ψS-</i>locus. The locus covered 874 kb across three scaffolds <i>Sc29</i>, <i>Sc276</i> and <i>Sc184</i>, possessing a considerably higher number of <i>SLF</i> genes than <i>S. lycopersicum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Zhou, J. et al. Structural and transcriptional analysis of S-locus F-box genes in Antirrhinum. Sex Plant Reprod. 16, 165–177 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d14004e1967">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Kubo, K. et al. Gene duplication and genetic exchange drive the evolution of S-RNase-based self-incompatibility in Petunia. Nat. Plants 1, 14005 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d14004e1970">35</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#Fig4">4a</a>). Six pseudogenes with FBA domains were inferred to be loss-of-function. No S-RNase was found in or near the locus, suggesting it might have been lost during horticultural selection for self-compatible <i>A. majus</i>. The <i>RAD</i> gene was located about 1 Mb upstream of <i>SLF1</i>, consistent with previous studies showing its linkage with the <i>S</i>-locus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Schwarz-Sommer, Z. et al. A linkage map of an F2 hybrid population of Antirrhinum majus and A. molle. Genetics 163, 699–710 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d14004e1990">36</a></sup>. Expression analysis showed that 30 <i>SLF</i> genes were expressed in either pollen or anthers indicating they could play a role in pollen function. The number of <i>SLF</i> genes in the <i>A. majus ψS-</i>locus is more than twice that found in <i>S. lycopersicum</i> (15 <i>SLF</i> genes including 11 pseudogenes)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Yang, Q., Zhang, D., Li, Q., Cheng, Z. &amp; Xue, Y. Heterochromatic and genetic features are consistent with recombination suppression of the self-incompatibility locus in Antirrhinum. Plant J. 51, 140–151 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2010">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Kubo, K. et al. Gene duplication and genetic exchange drive the evolution of S-RNase-based self-incompatibility in Petunia. Nat. Plants 1, 14005 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2013">35</a></sup> and that the <i>ψS</i>-locus of <i>A. majus</i> contains the largest number of active <i>SLF</i> genes annotated so far in a plant genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#Fig4">4b</a> and Supplementary Data Sets <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM3">9</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM3">11</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Genomic features of the ψS-locus of A. majus and its synteny with the S-locus regions of A. hispanicum."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Genomic features of the <i>ψS</i>-locus of <i>A. majus</i> and its synteny with the <i>S</i>-locus regions of <i>A. hispanicum</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0349-9/MediaObjects/41477_2018_349_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0349-9/MediaObjects/41477_2018_349_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="350" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Chromosomal locations of three scaffolds covering the <i>ψS</i>-locus region of <i>A. majus</i>. A genetic recombination map of chromosome 8 is shown on the top panel. The <i>x</i> axis shows its physical distance (Mb) and the <i>y</i> axis its genetic distance (cM). A schematic representation of chromosome 8 is shown in the middle panel with a red dot indicating its centromere. The <i>ψS</i>-locus is depicted as a blue box on its short arm. A vertical red line in the chromosome indicates the <i>RAD</i> gene. The lower panel shows three scaffolds of <i>Sc29</i>, <i>Sc276</i> and <i>Sc184</i> covering the <i>ψS</i>-locus region. <b>b</b>, Transcriptional profiles of the <i>ψS</i>-locus and its flanking regions of <i>A. majus</i>. The light orange shadow denotes the predicted <i>ψS</i>-locus region (<i>SLF1</i>–<i>SLF37</i>). This region between <i>RAD</i> and <i>SLF37</i> contains a total number of 102 annotated genes. The bottom panel is a schematic representation of the <i>SLF</i> genes. Orange squares indicate the <i>ψ</i><i>SLF</i> genes and green arrows the other annotated genes (I: a putative MYB family transcription factor; II and III, putative RNA-binding proteins and IV, a putative phosphate-dependent transferase). <b>c</b>, The synteny of the <i>S</i>-locus regions between <i>A. majus</i> and <i>S</i><sub><i>1</i></sub>, <i>S</i><sub><i>2</i></sub>, <i>S</i><sub><i>4</i></sub> and <i>S</i><sub><i>5</i></sub> haplotypes of <i>A. hispanicum</i>. Different colours indicate syntenic and inversion regions between the <i>ψS</i>-locus and <i>S</i><sub><i>1</i></sub> (<i>S</i><sub><i>1a</i></sub>, <i>S</i><sub><i>1b</i></sub> and <i>S</i><sub><i>1c</i></sub>), <i>S</i><sub><i>2</i></sub>, <i>S</i><sub><i>4</i></sub> (<i>S</i><sub><i>4a</i></sub>, <i>S</i><sub><i>4b</i></sub> and <i>S</i><sub><i>4c</i></sub>) or <i>S</i><sub><i>5</i></sub> (<i>S</i><sub><i>5a</i></sub> and <i>S</i><sub><i>5b</i></sub>) haplotypes of <i>A. hispanicum</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We compared the <i>ψS</i>-locus sequence with nine assembled TAC (transformation-competent artificial chromosome) sequences from four <i>S</i> haplotypes of self-incompatible <i>A. hispanicum</i>. Gene collinearity between <i>S</i>-alleles was revealed in the genomic region extending from <i>AhS</i><i>LF12</i> to <i>AhS</i><i>LF13</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#Fig4">4c</a>). An intrachromosome inversion around the <i>S</i>-locus was found to occur in the <i>S</i><sub><i>2</i></sub> haplotype of <i>A. hispanicum</i> as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Yang, Q., Zhang, D., Li, Q., Cheng, Z. &amp; Xue, Y. Heterochromatic and genetic features are consistent with recombination suppression of the self-incompatibility locus in Antirrhinum. Plant J. 51, 140–151 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2298">33</a></sup>. In contrast to the <i>ψS</i>-locus of <i>A.majus</i>, an <i>S-</i><i>RNase</i> gene was found in every sequenced <i>A. hispanicum S</i> allele, suggesting that the <i>S-</i><i>RNase</i> had been deleted in the <i>ψS</i>-locus. Notably, a pseudo-gene <i>AmSLF</i><i>18</i> in the <i>A. majus ψS</i>-locus had an orthologue in the <i>S</i><sub><i>4</i></sub> haplotype in <i>A. hispanicum</i>, which had a complete coding sequence and was expressed, suggesting the latter is an active gene in the <i>S</i>-locus and the former lost function, possibly following the loss of <i>S</i>-RNase (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#Fig4">4c</a> and Supplementary Data Set <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM3">11</a>). The orthologous <i>SLFs</i> among different haplotypes show a low allelic diversity, consistent with our previous finding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Zhou, J. et al. Structural and transcriptional analysis of S-locus F-box genes in Antirrhinum. Sex Plant Reprod. 16, 165–177 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2359">34</a></sup>.</p><p>The nonsynonymous (Ka) and synonymous (Ks) substitution rates of the 12 collinear <i>SLF</i> gene pairs showed that the values of <i>SLFs</i> are lower than that of <i>S-</i><i>RNase</i> in <i>Antirrhinum</i>, and the allelic <i>SLF</i> genes showed a ratio of Ka/Ks = 0.41, consistent with a negative frequency-dependent selection detected previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Kubo, K. et al. Gene duplication and genetic exchange drive the evolution of S-RNase-based self-incompatibility in Petunia. Nat. Plants 1, 14005 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2384">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Wright, S. The distribution of self-sterility alleles in populations. Genetics 24, 538–552 (1939)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2387">37</a></sup>. Only <i>SLF14</i> appears to be a positively selected gene (Ka/Ks &gt; 1) (Supplementary Data Set <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM3">12</a>). The average divergence time of these orthologous <i>SLF</i> genes was estimated to be 4 Ma, similar to an estimated early <i>Antirrhinum</i> species divergence time of less than 5.3 Ma<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Vieira, C. P., Vieira, J. &amp; Charlesworth, D. Evolution of the cycloidea gene family in Antirrhinum and Misopates. Mol. Biol. Evol. 16, 1474–1783 (1999)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2403">38</a></sup>. However, the average divergence time of the <i>S-</i><i>RNase</i><i>s</i> of <i>A. hispanicum</i> is estimated to be around 62–120 Ma, similar to the species divergence between <i>Antirrhinum</i> and Solanaceae species estimated in our study (Supplementary Data Set <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#MOESM3">13</a>). These results suggest that the divergence of S-RNase occurred before the WGD and they were well maintained in the lineage of <i>Antirrhinum</i>. Our results showed that a near-complete <i>ψS</i>-locus <i>A. majus</i> was identified.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>The genome sequence of <i>A. majus</i> reported here represents a sequenced genome of a species belonging to the family Plantaginaceae, and reveals a WGD specific to this family. Zygomorphy in the <i>Antirrhinum</i> lineage is thought to have arisen in the late Cretaceous period in the fossil record<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Vieira, C. P., Vieira, J. &amp; Charlesworth, D. Evolution of the cycloidea gene family in Antirrhinum and Misopates. Mol. Biol. Evol. 16, 1474–1783 (1999)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2449">38</a></sup>, as a mechanism facilitating insect-mediated pollination. We show that two key TCP genes controlling zygomorphy are collinear on a syntenic block generated by the WGD of the Plantaginaceae, suggesting that the duplication may have provided a genetic basis for the evolution of this trait. An independent WGD may have been involved in the evolution of zygomorphy in <i>Glycine</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Jabbour, F., Nadot, S. &amp; Damerval, C. Evolution of floral symmetry: a state of the art. C. R. Biol. 332, 219–231 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2455">39</a></sup>, and the missing type I Mβ MADS-box genes family resulted in bilaterally symmetrical flowers in the Orchidaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Zhang, G. et al. The Apostasia genome and the evolution of orchids. Nature 549, 379–383 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2459">40</a></sup>. The timing of the WGD event in the Plantaginaceae corresponds to the age of this family on the basis of the molecular dating approaches<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bell, C. D., Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. The age and diversification of the angiosperms re-revisited. Am. J. Bot. 97, 1296–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2464">25</a></sup>. WGD events have been considered to be catalysts for species diversification and evolutionary novelty in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Schranz, M. E., Mohammadin, S. &amp; Edger, P. P. Ancient whole genome duplications, novelty and diversification: the WGD Radiation Lag-Time Model. Curr. Opin. Plant. Biol. 15, 147–153 (2012)." href="#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2468">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Tank, D. C. et al. Nested radiations and the pulse of angiosperm diversification – increased diversification rates often follow whole genome duplications. New Phytol. 207, 454–467 (2015)." href="#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2468_1">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Soltis, P. S. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Ancient WGD events as drivers of key innovations in angiosperms. Curr. Opin. Plant. Biol. 30, 159–165 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2471">43</a></sup>. The availability of more species with completely sequenced genomes in the Plantaginaceae and their relatives may help clarify the role of the WGD in the expansion and evolution of the family.</p><p>The <i>Antirrhinum</i> genome also sheds light on the evolution of self-incompatiblity. The fine genomic structure of the <i>ψS</i>-locus from <i>A. majus</i> reveals a large number of pollen <i>SLFs</i>, probably due to gene duplication, recombination suppression, purifying selection and frequency-dependent selection associated with the <i>S</i>-locus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Kubo, K. et al. Gene duplication and genetic exchange drive the evolution of S-RNase-based self-incompatibility in Petunia. Nat. Plants 1, 14005 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2494">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Wright, S. The distribution of self-sterility alleles in populations. Genetics 24, 538–552 (1939)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2497">37</a></sup>. Relatively low allelic diversity was observed between orthologous <i>SLFs</i> among different haplotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Zhou, J. et al. Structural and transcriptional analysis of S-locus F-box genes in Antirrhinum. Sex Plant Reprod. 16, 165–177 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2504">34</a></sup>, compared with the paralogues within a haplotype, perhaps because extensive divergence would lead to recognition and self-inactivation of S-RNase resulting in loss of self-incompatibility. The deletion of S-RNase in cultivated <i>A. majus</i> could be responsible for the loss of self-incompatibility, giving an essentially irreversible transition. Such deletions may account for why self-compatible species are difficult or almost impossible to revert back to self-incompatible species (Doll’s Law)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Igic, B. &amp; Kohn, J. R. The distribution of plant mating systems: study bias against obligately outcrossing species. Evolution 60, 1098–1103 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2511">44</a></sup>. The high microcolinearity of the <i>S</i>-locus between self-incompatible and self-compatible <i>Antirrhinum</i> indicates that the deletion of <i>S-</i><i>RNase</i> in self-compatible species was a recent event. In fact, some mutated <i>SLF</i> genes in different haplotypes also arose recently<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Zhou, J. et al. Structural and transcriptional analysis of S-locus F-box genes in Antirrhinum. Sex Plant Reprod. 16, 165–177 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2530">34</a></sup>.</p><p>The physical size of the <i>S</i>-locus in <i>S. lycopersicum</i> is much larger than that in <i>A. majus</i> (17 Mb compared to 2 Mb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Schwarz-Sommer, Z. et al. A linkage map of an F2 hybrid population of Antirrhinum majus and A. molle. Genetics 163, 699–710 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2546">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Li, W. &amp; Chetelat, R. T. Unilateral incompatibility geneui1.1 encodes an S-locus F-box protein expressed in pollen of Solanum species. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 112, 4417–4422 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2549">45</a></sup>, yet it contains fewer <i>SLF</i> genes (17 compared to 37). Less repetitive sequences are found in the <i>ψS</i>-locus and <i>S</i> loci of <i>Antirrhinum</i> compared with that of <i>Solanum</i>, suggesting that an increase of the gene numbers through unequal crossovers possibly results in the <i>Antirrhinum S</i>-locus, and that repetitive element enrichments could underlie the large physical size and low gene density of the <i>S</i>-locus of <i>Solanum</i>, enhanced perhaps by its centromeric location.</p><p>In conclusion, the assembled <i>A. majus</i> sequence provides a reference genome for the Plantaginaceae and will be helpful for genetic, genomic and evolutionary studies in both <i>Antirrhinum</i> and other flowering plants. For example, studies on a natural hybrid zone between <i>Antirrhinum</i> species using this genome sequence as a reference have revealed patterns of selection and gene flow underlying the evolution of flower colour pattern<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Bradley, D. et al. Evolution of flower color pattern through selection on regulatory small RNAs. Science 358, 925–928 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2591">46</a></sup>. We hope the resource will be a useful stimulus to further studies.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Plant materials</h3><p>The seeds of cultivated <i>Antirrhinum</i> (<i>A. majus</i> JI7) were surface-sterilized and plated on Murashige–Skoog (1/2 MS) plates (×1/2 MS salts, 0.23% phytagel and ×1 Gamborg’s B5 vitamin mixture, all from Sigma) and grown in growth chambers (160 h/8 h, light/dark) with white fluorescent light (100 μmol m<sup>−2</sup> s<sup>-1</sup>) at 22 °C. After avoiding light for 72 h, we harvested leaf tissues and extracted DNA using the cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB) method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Allen, G. C., FloreVergara, M. A., Krasynanski, S., Kumar, S. &amp; Thompson, W. F. A modified protocol for rapid DNA isolation from plant tissues using cetyltrimethylammonium bromide. Nat. Protoc. 1, 2320–2325 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2617">47</a></sup>.</p><p>To generate the RILs, <i>A. majus</i> JI7 stock (TA7–7) was crossed to <i>A. charidemi</i> (TA1282). The <i>A. charidemi</i> individual was derived from accession Ac1024 -Y-TES -1, with seed collected from Cabo de Gata Spain. A single F<sub>1</sub> hybrid (P107-2) was self-pollinated to produce a total of 195 F<sub>2</sub> plants. Each plant was self-pollinated to produce a unique RIL. A total of 48 RILs were eventually developed from single F<sub>2</sub> individuals taken through additional rounds of self-pollination through to the F<sub>7</sub> to F<sub>9</sub> generation. <i>A. hispanicum</i> lines (<i>AhS</i><sub><i>2</i></sub><i>S</i><sub><i>4</i></sub> and <i>AhS</i><sub><i>1</i></sub><i>S</i><sub><i>5</i></sub>) were maintained by vegetative cuttings as described by Xue et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Xue, Y., Carpenter, R., Dickinson, H. &amp; Coen, E. Origin of allelic diversity in Antirrhinmm S locus RNases. Plant Cell 8, 805–814 (1996)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2670">48</a></sup>. and were originally sourced from the Gatersleben collection (http://www.ipk-gatersleben.de/en/gbisipk-gaterslebendegbis-i/).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">WGS</h3><p>High-quality genomic DNA was extracted from young leaves of cultivated <i>A. majus</i> JI7 using the CTAB method. According to the manufacturer’s instructions (Illumina HiSeq 2000), we constructed a total of 2 × 100 paired-end sequencing libraries with insert sizes from 170 bp to 20 kb for standard WGS sequencing. For small-insert (&lt;2 kb) libraries, DNA was fragmented, end repaired, ligated to Illumina paired-end adaptors, size selected and purified by PCR amplification. For large-insert (≥2 kb) mate-paired libraries, about 20–50 μg genomic DNA was fragmented, and biotin-labelled adaptors were annealed to the fragment ends before self-ligation to form circularized DNA. This library was re-fragmented and target sequences (that is, the long molecule ends) were enriched using biotin/streptavidin, and then prepared for sequencing. All of the above libraries were sequenced on Illumina Genome Analyzer sequencing platforms. In total, we generated about 90.85 Gb (roughly ×144.24) reads. Using the Pacific Biosciences (PacBio) platform for single-molecule, real-time (SMRT) sequencing we generated a total of 25.89 Gb from 30 SMRT cells, with an average subread length of 5.2 kb and a N50 size of 13.4 kb. The 48 individual RILs were genotyped using the WGS sequencing. We obtained a total of 201.49 Gb sequencing data and the average sequencing depth of each sample was 4.5 Gb (×8.2). 92.40% of the reads could be mapped into the genome.</p><p>The genome size was evaluated using the total length of sequence reads divided by sequencing depth as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Li, R. et al. The sequence and de novo assembly of the giant panda genome. Nature 463, 311–317 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2688">49</a></sup>. To estimate the sequencing depth, we counted the frequency of each 17-mer from the Illumina WGS sequencing reads and plotted the distribution of copy numbers. The peak value of the frequency curve represents the overall sequencing depth. We used the algorithm (<i>N</i> × (<i>L</i> − <i>K</i> + 1) − <i>B</i>)<i>/D</i> = <i>G</i>, where <i>N</i> is the total sequence read number, <i>L</i> the average length of sequence reads and <i>K</i> the length defined as 17–31 bp here. To minimize the influence of sequencing error, <i>K</i>-mers with low frequency (&lt;4) are discarded. <i>B</i> is the total number of low-frequency <i>K</i>-mers. <i>G</i> denotes the genome size and <i>D</i> is the overall depth estimated from <i>K</i>-mer distribution.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genome assembly</h3><p>The assembly was performed on HPC (High Performance Computing) system with 40 nodes, each one having 16 CPU cores and 128 GB of RAM. The operating system was Centos 6.3 64-bit (Linux). We corrected the PacBio long reads using the Canu pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Koren, S., Walenz, B. P., Berlin, K., Miller, J. R. &amp; Phillippy, A. M. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2747">19</a></sup>, and then assembled them into contigs (N50 = 733 kb; total length = 510 Mb). The Canu pipeline parameters were: genome size = 600 Mb, error rate = 0.013. We then further polished the PacBio assembled contigs using Quiver<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Li, R. et al. The sequence and de novo assembly of the giant panda genome. Nature 463, 311–317 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2751">49</a></sup>. We used the mate-pair sequences to connect the contig sequences with SSPACE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Marten, B. &amp; Walter, P. SSPACE-LongRead: scaffolding bacterial draft genomes using long read sequence information. BMC Bioinformatics 15, 211 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2755">20</a></sup>. Initially, we required 30 connections to support connection of contig sequences into a scaffold. We then repeated this process iteratively using the result of the scaffolding as input but reducing the connection support by five. The final assembly spans were produced with the connection support parameter set to 10.</p><p>To construct the linkage map and organize scaffolds into pseudochromosomes, we resequenced individual RILs and their parents. The raw reads generated from the Illumina-Pipeline included low-quality, adaptor contaminated and duplicated reads. Reads were filtered using Trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: A flexible trimmer for Illumina Sequence Data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2762">50</a></sup> with default parameters, retaining only reads longer than 50 bp after quality trimming. We used BWA-mem<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at arXiv: &#xA;                    https://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0349-9#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d14004e2766">51</a></sup> (<a href="http://bio-bwa.sourceforge.net/">http://bio-bwa.sourceforge.net/</a>) with default settings to align filtered reads to the assembled genome. After alignment, we used SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools.
